<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome of Prasinoderma coloniale unveils the existence of a third phylum within green plants"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Ecology &amp; Evolution 2020 4:9"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-06-22"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Genome analysis of the pico-eukaryotic marine green alga Prasinoderma coloniale CCMP 1413 unveils the existence of a novel phylum within green plants (Viridiplantae), the Prasinodermophyta, which diverged before the split of Chlorophyta and Streptophyta. Structural features of the genome and gene family comparisons revealed an intermediate position of the P. coloniale genome (25.3&#8201;Mb) between the extremely compact, small genomes of picoplanktonic Mamiellophyceae (Chlorophyta) and the larger, more complex genomes of early-diverging streptophyte algae. Reconstruction of the minimal core genome of Viridiplantae allowed identification of an ancestral toolkit of transcription factors and flagellar proteins. Adaptations of P. coloniale to its deep-water, oligotrophic environment involved expansion of light-harvesting proteins, reduction of early light-induced proteins, evolution of a distinct type of C4 photosynthesis and carbon-concentrating mechanism, synthesis of the metal-complexing metabolite picolinic acid, and vitamin B1, B7 and B12 auxotrophy. The P. coloniale genome provides first insights into the dawn of green plant evolution. Genome analysis of the pico-eukaryotic marine green alga Prasinoderma coloniale CCMP 1413 unveils the existence of a novel phylum within green plants (Viridiplantae), the Prasinodermophyta, which diverged before the split of Chlorophyta and Streptophyta."/>

                    <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">The genome of <i>Prasinoderma coloniale</i> unveils the existence of a third phylum within green plants</h1>
                    <ul class="c-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Genome analysis of the pico-eukaryotic marine green alga <i>Prasinoderma coloniale</i> CCMP 1413 unveils the existence of a novel phylum within green plants (Viridiplantae), the Prasinodermophyta, which diverged before the split of Chlorophyta and Streptophyta. Structural features of the genome and gene family comparisons revealed an intermediate position of the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome (25.3 Mb) between the extremely compact, small genomes of picoplanktonic Mamiellophyceae (Chlorophyta) and the larger, more complex genomes of early-diverging streptophyte algae. Reconstruction of the minimal core genome of Viridiplantae allowed identification of an ancestral toolkit of transcription factors and flagellar proteins. Adaptations of <i>P. coloniale</i> to its deep-water, oligotrophic environment involved expansion of light-harvesting proteins, reduction of early light-induced proteins, evolution of a distinct type of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis and carbon-concentrating mechanism, synthesis of the metal-complexing metabolite picolinic acid, and vitamin B<sub>1</sub>, B<sub>7</sub> and B<sub>12</sub> auxotrophy. The <i>P. coloniale</i> genome provides first insights into the dawn of green plant evolution.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>One of the most important biological events in the history of life was the successful colonization of the terrestrial landscape by green plants (Viridiplantae) that paved the way for terrestrial animal evolution, altering geomorphology and changes in the Earth’s climate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Niklas, K. J. The Evolutionary Biology of Plants (Univ. of Chicago Press, 1997)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39070e924">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kenrick, P. &amp; Crane, P. The origin and early evolution of plants on Land. Nature 389, 33–39 (1997)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39070e924_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Willis, K. &amp; McElwain, J. The Evolution of Plants (Oxford Univ. Press, 2014)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39070e927">3</a></sup>. The Viridiplantae comprise perhaps 500,000 species, ranging from the smallest to the largest eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Judd, W. S., Campbell, C. S., Kellogg, E. A., Stevens, P. F. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. Plant Systematics: A Phylogenetic Approach (Sinauer, 2008)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39070e931">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Courties, C. et al. Smallest eukaryotic organism. Nature 370, 255 (1994)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39070e934">5</a></sup>. Divergence time estimates from molecular data suggest that Viridiplantae may be close to 1 billion years old<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Yoon, H. S., Hackett, J. D., Ciniglia, C., Pinto, G. &amp; Bhattacharya, D. A molecular timeline for the origin of photosynthetic eukaryotes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 21, 809–818 (2004)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39070e938">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E2274–E2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39070e941">7</a></sup>. All extant green plants are classified in either of two divisions/phyla, Chlorophyta and Streptophyta, which differ structurally, biochemically and molecularly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bremer, K. Summary of green plant phylogeny and classification. Cladistics 1, 369–385 (1985)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39070e945">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Melkonian, M. &amp; Surek, B. Phylogeny of the Chlorophyta: congruence between ultrastructural and molecular evidence. Bull. Soc. Zool. Fr. 120, 191–208 (1995)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39070e945_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lewis, L. A. &amp; McCourt, R. M. Green algae and the origin of land plants. Am. J. Bot. 91, 1535–1556 (2004)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39070e945_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Becker, B. &amp; Marin, B. Streptophyte algae and the origin of embryophytes. Ann. Bot. 103, 999–1004 (2009)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39070e945_3">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Phylogeny and molecular evolution of the green algae. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 1–46 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39070e948">12</a></sup>. The Streptophyta contain the land plants (embryophytes) and a paraphyletic assemblage of algae known as the streptophyte algae, whereas all other green algae comprise the Chlorophyta. The reconstruction of phylogenetic relationships across green plants using transcriptomic or genomic data provided evidence that unicellular, often scaly, flagellate organisms were positioned near the base of the radiation in both phyla<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39070e952">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ruhfel, B. R., Gitzendanner, M. A., Soltis, P. S., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Burleigh, J. G. From algae to angiosperms–inferring the phylogeny of green plants (Viridiplantae) from 360 plastid genomes. BMC Evol. Biol. 14, 23 (2014)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39070e952_1">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gitzendanner, M. A., Soltis, P. S., Wong, G. K. S., Ruhfel, B. R. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Plastid phylogenomic analysis of green plants: a billion years of evolutionary history. Am. J. Bot. 105, 291–301 (2018)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39070e952_2">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Turmel, M. &amp; Lemieux, C. Evolution of the plastid genome in green algae. Adv. Bot. Res. 85, 157–193 (2018)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39070e955">16</a></sup>, corroborating earlier proposals based on ultrastructural analyses that the common ancestor of all green plants may have been a scaly flagellate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Stewart, K. D. &amp; Mattox, K. R. Structural evolution in the flagellated cells of green algae and land plants. BioSystems 10, 145–152 (1978)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39070e960">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Melkonian, M. Structural and evolutionary aspects of the flagellar apparatus in green algae and land plants. Taxon 31, 255–265 (1982)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39070e963">18</a></sup>. The search for an extant relative of such a flagellate, however, has been in vain, although an initial report suggested that <i>Mesostigma viride</i> diverged before the split of Chlorophyta and Streptophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. Ancestral chloroplast genome in Mesostigma viride reveals an early branch of green plant evolution. Nature 403, 649–652 (2000)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39070e970">19</a></sup>, a result not corroborated by later studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wang, S. et al. Genomes of early-diverging streptophyte algae shed light on plant terrestrialization. Nat. Plants 6, 95–106 (2020)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39070e974">20</a></sup>. <i>M. viride</i> is now recognized as an early-diverging member of the Streptophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rodríguez-Ezpeleta, N., Philippe, H., Brinkmann, H., Becker, B. &amp; Melkonian, M. Phylogenetic analyses of nuclear, mitochondrial, and plastid multigene data sets support the placement of Mesostigma in the Streptophyta. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 723–731 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39070e981">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Marin, B. &amp; Melkonian, M. Mesostigmatophyceae, a new class of streptophyte green algae revealed by SSU rRNA sequence comparisons. Protist 150, 399–417 (1999)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39070e984">22</a></sup>. While the majority of the early-diverging lineages in the Chlorophyta consisted of (mostly marine) scaly flagellates, some lineages were represented by very small, non-flagellate unicells often surrounded by cell walls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Guillou, L. et al. Diversity of picoplanktonic prasinophytes assessed by direct nuclear SSU rDNA sequencing of environmental samples and novel isolates retrieved from oceanic and coastal marine ecosystems. Protist 155, 193–214 (2004)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39070e989">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Marin, B. &amp; Melkonian, M. Molecular phylogeny and classification of the Mamiellophyceae class. nov. (Chlorophyta) based on sequence comparisons of the nuclear- and plastid-encoded rRNA operons. Protist 161, 304–336 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39070e992">24</a></sup>. One of these lineages, provisionally termed ‘Prasinococcales’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Guillou, L. et al. Diversity of picoplanktonic prasinophytes assessed by direct nuclear SSU rDNA sequencing of environmental samples and novel isolates retrieved from oceanic and coastal marine ecosystems. Protist 155, 193–214 (2004)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39070e996">23</a></sup> (clade VI), could not be reliably positioned in phylogenetic trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Marin, B. &amp; Melkonian, M. Molecular phylogeny and classification of the Mamiellophyceae class. nov. (Chlorophyta) based on sequence comparisons of the nuclear- and plastid-encoded rRNA operons. Protist 161, 304–336 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1000">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. Six newly sequenced chloroplast genomes from prasinophyte green algae provide insights into the relationships among prasinophyte lineages and the diversity of streamlined genome architecture in picoplanktonic species. BMC Genomics 15, 857 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1003">25</a></sup>. A major step forward was made when it was discovered that an enigmatic, non-cultured group of deep-water, oceanic macroscopic algae of palmelloid organization comprising the genera <i>Verdigellas</i> and <i>Palmophyllum</i> formed a deeply diverging lineage of Viridiplantae that included the Prasinococcales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zechman, F. W. et al. An unrecognized ancient lineage of green plants persists in deep marine waters. J. Phycol. 46, 1288–1295 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1013">26</a></sup>. Later, the class Palmophyllophyceae was established for these organisms as the first divergence in Chlorophyta, that is sister to all other Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1018">27</a></sup>. Phylogenies based on nuclear-encoded ribosomal RNA genes (4,579 positions), however, placed Palmophyllophyceae as the earliest divergence in Viridiplantae, but monophyly of Chlorophyta + Streptophyta to the exclusion of Palmophyllophyceae, received no support in these analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1022">27</a></sup>.</p><p>To date, genomic resources for the Palmophyllophyceae have been limited to organelle genomes. Here we present the first nuclear genome sequence of a unicellular member of this lineage, <i>Prasinoderma coloniale</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig1">1a</a>). Based on phylogenomic analyses, we establish a new phylum for this group, the Prasinodermophyta, with two classes, as the earliest divergence of the Viridiplantae. The genome of <i>P. coloniale</i> provided new insights into pico-eukaryotic biology near the dawn of green plant evolution.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of P. coloniale."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Phylogenetic analysis of <i>P. coloniale</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="547" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Light micrograph of <i>P. coloniale</i>. <b>b</b>, The phylogenetic tree was constructed using the maximum-likelihood method in RAxML and MrBayes based on a concatenated sequence alignment of 256 single-copy genes (500 bootstraps). <b>c</b>, The basal divergence of the new phylum Prasinodermophyta, as revealed by analyses of complete nuclear- and plastid-encoded rRNA operons from 109 Archaeplastida. The rRNA dataset comprised 8,818 aligned positions and contained representatives of all major lineages of Rhodoplantae (seven classes), Glaucoplantae (four genera) and Viridiplantae (three divisions with several classes) including embryophytes. Shown is the RAxML phylogeny (GTRGAMMA model); the three support values at branches are RAxML/IQ-TREE bootstrap percentages/Bayesian posterior probabilities. Bold branches received maximal support (100/100/1).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and characteristics</h3><p>The genome size of <i>P. coloniale</i> was estimated to be about 26.04 Mb. After reads filtering (7.4 Gb PacBio data) and self-correction, a 25.3 Mb genome was de novo assembled consisting of 22 chromosomes, including the complete chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">2</a>). The sizes of the individual chromosomes varied from 0.45 to 3.60 Mb. BUSCO analysis showed a high degree of completeness of the genome, with 282 out of 303 (93.1%) complete eukaryotic universal genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">1</a>). Additionally, 99.38% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">2</a>) of the transcriptome could be mapped to the assembled genome. <i>P. coloniale</i> has a GC content of 69.8%, while 6.51% of the genome consists of repeats (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">3</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig7">1</a>). A total of 7,139 protein-coding genes were annotated, of which 6,996 were supported by the transcriptome. Additionally, 6,759 (94.7%) genes were annotated from known protein databases (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Phylogenetic analyses and Prasinodermophyta div. nov</h3><p>Phylogenetic analyses of <i>P. coloniale</i> were performed with two different taxon and datasets. (1) Both maximum-likelihood and Bayesian trees were constructed from an alignment of 256 orthologues of single-copy nuclear genes from 28 taxa of Archaeplastida, showing that <i>P. coloniale</i> (and the related <i>Prasinococcus capsulatus</i>) diverged before the split of Streptophyta and Chlorophyta (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig1">1b</a>). All internal branches in the tree received maximal/nearly maximal support, and the monophyly of Streptophyta + Chlorophyta to the exclusion of <i>P. coloniale</i> and <i>P. capsulatus</i> received 88% bootstrap and 1.0 posterior probability support. A phylogenetic tree constructed from 31 mitochondrial genes of 19 taxa of Archaeplastida also revealed <i>P. coloniale</i> as the earliest divergence in Viridiplantae (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">4</a>). (2) We increased the taxon sampling to 109 taxa of Archaeplastida, including six sequences of Palmophyllophyceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1149">27</a></sup> comprising nuclear- and plastid-encoded rRNA operons. The phylogeny corroborated the multi-protein phylogeny because Palmophyllophyceae again diverged before the split of Chlorophyta and Streptophyta (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig1">1c</a>). Separate phylogenies of nuclear- and plastid-encoded rRNA operons gave congruent results, although support values were generally lower (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">6</a>). The summary coalescent method ASTRAL gave inconclusive results (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">5</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">8</a>), and taxon sampling was sensitive to long-branch attraction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Molloy, E. &amp; Warnow, T. Large-scale species tree estimation. Preprint at arXiv &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1904.02600&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1172">28</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">9</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig8">2</a>). The former tree is corroborated by a recent phylotranscriptomic analysis of 1,090 viridiplant species in which the placement of three Palmophyllophyceae was unstable in ASTRAL trees but resolved as the basal divergence of Viridiplantae in concatenated trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Leebens-Mack, J. H. et al. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature 574, 679–685 (2019)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1183">29</a></sup>. Previous plastome phylogenies placed Palmophyllophyceae either as the earliest divergence within Chlorophyta, sister to all other Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1187">27</a></sup>, or in an unresolved position among Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gitzendanner, M. A., Soltis, P. S., Wong, G. K. S., Ruhfel, B. R. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Plastid phylogenomic analysis of green plants: a billion years of evolutionary history. Am. J. Bot. 105, 291–301 (2018)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1191">15</a></sup>. Plastome phylogenies are limited by the dataset (70–80 plastid-encoded genes) but also suffer from introgression of the plastid from one species to another, recombination and gene conversion, as well as differential selective pressures acting on protein-coding plastid genes, which may also introduce biases and lead to incongruent gene and species trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Marin, B. Nested in the Chlorellales or independent class? Phylogeny and classification of the Pedinophyceae (Viridiplantae) revealed by molecular phylogenetic analyses of complete nuclear and plastid-encoded rRNA operons. Protist 163, 778–805 (2012)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1195">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shen, X.-X., Hittinger, C. T. &amp; Rokas, A. Contentious relationships in phylogenomic studies can be driven by a handful of genes. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0126 (2017)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1195_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Walker, J. F., Walker-Hale, N., Vargas, O. M., Larson, D. A. &amp; Stull, G. W. Characterizing gene tree conflict in plastome-inferred phylogenies. PeerJ. 7, e7747 (2019)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1195_2">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gonçalves, D. J. P., Simpson, B. B., Ortiz, E. M., Shimizu, G. H. &amp; Jansen, R. K. Incongruence between gene trees and species trees and phylogenetic signal variation in plastid genes. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 138, 219–232 (2019)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1198">33</a></sup>. For example, unlike nuclear trees, some studies have failed to recover Ulvophyceae, Trebouxiophyceae and Pedinophyceae as monophyletic groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1202">27</a></sup> or Mesostigmatophyceae within Streptophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Gitzendanner, M. A., Soltis, P. S., Wong, G. K. S., Ruhfel, B. R. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Plastid phylogenomic analysis of green plants: a billion years of evolutionary history. Am. J. Bot. 105, 291–301 (2018)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1207">15</a></sup>. Based on their phylogenetic positions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig1">1b,c</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">4</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">6</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig8">2</a>), gene family comparisons and molecular synapomorphies, we here propose a new division/phylum for the Palmophyllophyceae sensu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1223">27</a></sup>, the Prasinodermophyta div. nov. with two classes, Prasinodermophyceae class. nov. and Palmophyllophyceae emend (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM4">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparison of gene families among Archaeplastida</h3><p>The phylogenetic placement of Prasinodermophyta as a sister group to all other Viridiplantae provided a unique opportunity to reconstruct the minimum core genome of Viridiplantae, and to compare the genome of <i>P. coloniale</i> to those of early-diverging Streptophyta, Chlorophyta and the Glaucoplantae, to identify plesiomorphic and apomorphic traits. In total, 4,052 orthogroups are shared among Chlorophyta and Streptophyta, of which 3,292 are also shared with <i>P. coloniale</i>. If the orthogroups shared uniquely by <i>P. coloniale</i> with either <i>Micromonas commoda</i> (621) or <i>Chlorokybus atmophyticus</i> (179) are added, 4,092 orthogroups represent the minimal core genome of Viridiplantae (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig2">2a</a>). A total of 1,356 unique orthogroups were found in <i>P. coloniale</i>, mainly involved in biological process categories such as photosynthesis-antenna proteins, plant–pathogen interaction and plant hormone signal transduction (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">6</a>). Thus, it is reasonable to expect that these unique biological traits reflect adaptations of <i>P. coloniale</i> to its deep-water/low-light, oligotrophic habitat.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparative analysis of P. coloniale and other Chlorophyta."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Comparative analysis of <i>P. coloniale</i> and other Chlorophyta.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="618" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Venn diagram showing unique and shared orthogroups among <i>P. coloniale</i>, <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, <i>M. commoda</i> and <i>C. paradoxa</i>. Gene numbers are given in parentheses. <b>b</b>, Percentages of proteins found among Viridiplantae (red), Chlorophyta-specific (blue) and Streptophyta-specific (green) based on the classification given in OrthoFinder. Species abbreviations are listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">32</a>. <b>c</b>, PCA of the type and number of Pfam domains of all genes across the Viridiplantae. <b>d</b>, Box-and-whisker plots depicting distributions of the lengths of exons and introns in selected Viridiplantae.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Comparative genomics of <i>P. coloniale</i> with early-diverging Viridiplantae</h3><p>About 38.5% of the <i>P. coloniale</i> genes gave best hits with Chlorophyta, while a similar percentage (33.9%) gave best hits with Streptophyta, supporting an equidistant relationship between <i>P. coloniale</i> and Streptophyta and Chlorophyta (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">10</a>). <i>P. coloniale</i>, along with some representative early-diverging Viridiplantae, showed a very similar percentage of Viridiplantae genes (commonly shared). The remaining proteins of <i>P. coloniale</i> were equally distributed among Streptophyta- and Chlorophyta-specific genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig2">2b</a>). Principal component analysis (PCA) showed that early-diverging Chlorophyta (Mamiellophyceae), streptophyte algae (Mesostigmatophyceae, Klebsormidiophyceae and Charophyceae), Glaucoplantae and Rhodoplantae form four separate clusters with <i>P. coloniale</i> in an isolated position, which also further supports its classification as a new and independent clade—that is, the Prasinodermophyta div. nov. (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig2">2c</a>)</p><p>Furthermore, a comparative analysis on structural genomic features showed a trend of gradually increasing average intron length and decreasing average exon length from <i>P. coloniale</i> to early-diverging streptophytes, and the opposite trend from <i>P. coloniale</i> to early-diverging Chlorophyta was observed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig2">2d</a>). In addition, the genome size, gene size, gene spacing distance and total and average exon numbers exhibited a similar pattern with early-diverging Chlorophyta (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig9">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">7</a>). However, the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome contains 41% coding sequences, higher than the early-diverging streptophytes but considerably lower than early-diverging Chlorophyta. In summary, the structural characteristics of the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome revealed its intermediate position between the extremely compact and small genomes of picoplanktonic early-diverging Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Grimsley, N., Yau, S., Piganeau, G. &amp; Moreau, H. in Marine Protists (eds. Ohtsuka, S. et al.) 107–127 (Springer, 2015)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1380">34</a></sup> and the larger and structurally more complex genomes of early-diverging streptophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1384">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1387">36</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Analysis of transcription factors in <i>P. coloniale</i>
</h3><p>In total, 55 of 114 types of TF/TR genes were identified in the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">8</a>). Although all 55 types of transcription factor/transcription regulator (TF/TR) genes of <i>P. coloniale</i> were also found in Chlorophyta and early-diverging streptophyte algae, considerably lower numbers of TF/TR genes (201) were identified in <i>P. coloniale</i> compared to Chlorophyta and Streptophyta (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">9</a>).</p><p>Among the 55 types of TF/TR genes, the majority (50) are also present in Glaucoplantae and/or Rhodoplantae, suggesting that these constituted the basic TF/TR toolbox in the common ancestor of Archaeplastida. However, five TF/TR types of <i>P. coloniale</i> (C2C2-Dof, WRKY, SBP, GARP_ARR-B and TAZ) were presumably gained in the common ancestor of the Viridiplantae since they are absent in both Glaucoplantae and Rhodoplantae (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">8</a>). WRKY proteins are key regulators of development, carbohydrate synthesis, senescence and responses to biotic and abiotic stresses in embryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Rinerson, C. I., Rabara, R. C., Tripathi, P., Shen, Q. J. &amp; Rushton, P. J. The evolution of WRKY transcription factors. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 66 (2015)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1428">37</a></sup>. Using newly retrieved WRKY sequences, we confirmed the presence of eight well-supported WRKY domain subgroups in Viridiplantae (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig10">4</a>). The number of gene copies with WRKY domains and the divergent sequences of the N-terminal WRKY domains in <i>P. coloniale</i> may be related to its picoplanktonic lifestyle and/or low-light environment (the picoplanktonic Mamiellophyceae generally also display more than one WRKY gene copy; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">8</a>).</p><p>The type-B phospho-accepting response regulator (GARP_ARR-B) family modulates plastid biogenesis, circadian clock oscillation, cytokinin signalling and control of the phosphate starvation response in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Safi, A. et al. The world according to GARP transcription factors. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 39, 159–167 (2017)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1445">38</a></sup>. Since many genes of the cytokinin biosynthesis and signalling pathways are lacking in <i>P. coloniale</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">10</a>), these response regulators may be involved in other functions. Finally, the evolution of the SQUAMOSA promotor-binding protein (SBP)-box TF was previously suggested to predate the split of Streptophyta and Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Guo, A.-Y. et al. Genome-wide identification and evolutionary analysis of the plant-specific SBP-box transcription factor family. Gene 418, 1–8 (2008)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1455">39</a></sup>. SBP-box TFs have diverse specialized functions in embryophytes, but in green algae they may be involved in more basic functions such as regulation of trace metal homeostasis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kropat, J. et al. A regulator of nutritional copper signaling in Chlamydomonas is an SBP domain protein that recognizes the GTAC core of copper response element. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 18730–18735 (2005)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1459">40</a></sup>. The C2C2-Dof (DNA binding with one finger) TFs have been implicated in light control of zygote germination in <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Moreno-Risueno, M. Á., Martínez, M., Vicente-Carbajosa, J. &amp; Carbonero, P. The family of DOF transcription factors: from green unicellular algae to vascular plants. Mol. Genet. Genomics 277, 379–390 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1466">41</a></sup> and apparently originated also in the common ancestor of Viridiplantae. Besides the five TF/TR gains, expansion in gene copy numbers was observed in only one TF (Jumonji_Other) in the common ancestor of Viridiplantae when compared with Glaucoplantae and Rhodoplantae. Plant JmjC domain-containing proteins have important functions in both histone modification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Crevillén, P. et al. Epigenetic reprogramming that prevents transgenerational inheritance of the vernalized state. Nature 515, 587–590 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1470">42</a></sup> and regulation of development and environmental responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Liu, C., Lu, F., Cui, X. &amp; Cao, X. Histone methylation in higher plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 61, 395–420 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1474">43</a></sup>.</p><p>In contrast to gains and expansion of TFs/TRs, <i>P. coloniale</i> also exhibited loss of six TFs/TRs (C2H2, C3H, CCAAT_HAP2, MADS_MIKC, MBF1 and Zinc Finger MIZ type) that are present in Chlorophyta, Streptophyta, Glaucoplantae and Rhodoplantae. Mapping of TFs/TRs on the phylogeny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig1">1b</a>) also allowed tentative conclusions about gains of TFs/TRs in the common ancestor of Chlorophyta + Streptophyta (five: ABI3/VP1, Dicer, HD_DDT, Pseudo ARR-B and Whirly) and the common ancestor of Streptophyta (seven: HD-ZIP_I_II, HD-ZIP_III, HD-PLINC, GRF, LUG, SRS and Trihelix).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Light-harvesting complex (LHC) and LHC-like proteins in <i>P. coloniale</i>
</h3><p>Archaeplastida produce metabolic energy by collecting solar energy and transferring it to photosynthetic reaction centres, facilitated by two types of light-harvesting complexes (LHC I and LHC II), composed of LHC proteins that interact with light-harvesting pigments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Croce, R., Van Grondelle, R., Van Amerongen, H. &amp; Van Stokkum, I. Light Harvesting in Photosynthesis (CRC Press, 2018)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1499">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Grossman, A. R., Bhaya, D., Apt, K. E. &amp; Kehoe, D. M. Light-harvesting complexes in oxygenic photosynthesis: diversity, control, and evolution. Annu. Rev. Genet. 29, 231–288 (1995)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1499_1">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dreyfuss, B. W. &amp; Thornber, J. P. Assembly of the light-harvesting complexes (LHCs) of photosystem II (monomeric LHC Iib complexes are intermediates in the formation of oligomeric LHC IIb complexes). Plant Physiol. 106, 829–839 (1994)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1499_2">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Schmid, V. H. R. Light-harvesting complexes of vascular plants. Cell. Mol. Life Sci. 65, 3619–3639 (2008)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1502">47</a></sup>. We identified 41 LHC and LHC-like proteins of <i>P. coloniale</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">11</a>).</p><p>Phylogenetic analysis of LHC proteins from <i>P. coloniale</i> showed them to be widely distributed in seven of the ten LHC clades, namely LHCA, LHCB, LHCX, PSBS, OHP, Ferrochelatase and ELIPs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig3">3</a>). The <i>P. coloniale</i> genome has 19 <i>Lhcb</i> genes (six of which apparently originated from three successive gene duplications in the <i>Prasinoderma</i> lineage). <i>P. coloniale</i> also displayed nine <i>Lhca</i> genes, whereas in most of the investigated early-diverging Chlorophyta/Mamiellophyceae and in <i>Mesostigma</i> (Streptophyta) there are only six <i>Lhca</i> genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">11</a>). As in the early-diverging Mamiellophyceae, <i>P. coloniale</i> displayed two LHCX proteins. There are three helix proteins in <i>P. coloniale</i>, as in <i>Cyanophora paradoxa</i>. Other types of LHC-like proteins, such as RedCap, SEP (SEP apparently originated in streptophyte algae) and LHCL, are missing in <i>P. coloniale</i>. The relatively large number of gene copies of chlorophyll-a/b-binding proteins (<i>Lhca</i>, <i>Lhcb)</i> in <i>P. coloniale</i> could reflect adaptation to the low-light environment from which this strain was isolated (150 m depth), requiring larger LHC antennae. This is corroborated by two other observations: first, the relatively low Chl-a/b ratio (1.13) reported for this organism and the related <i>Palmophyllum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Kunugi, M. et al. Evolution of green plants accompanied changes in light-harvesting systems. Plant Cell Physiol. 57, 1231–1243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1571">48</a></sup> (0.64), and second, the lower number (six) of ELIPs in <i>P. coloniale</i> compared to core Chlorophyta (nine in <i>Ulva lactuca</i> and ten in <i>C. reinhardtii)</i> or early-diverging subaerial/terrestrial streptophyte algae (13/9 in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> and <i>Klebsormidium nitens</i>, respectively).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogenetic tree of the LHC antenna protein superfamily."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Phylogenetic tree of the LHC antenna protein superfamily.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="591" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The tree is derived from a MAFFT alignment and was constructed using IQ-TREE (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Sec15">Methods</a>) with the model of sequence evolution suggested by the programme. Bootstrap values (500 replicates) ≥50% are shown. The LHC superfamily can be divided into ten clades, marked by different colours; the LHC genes of <i>P. coloniale</i> are highlighted in red. The coloured circles on the outer ring denote the distribution of the different LHC subfamilies in the respective taxa. The TUC clade comprises Trebouxiophyceae, Ulvophyceae and Chlorophyceae (all in Chlorophyta). ELIP, early light-induced protein; SEP, two-helix stress-enhanced protein; OHP, one-helix protein; PSBS, the photosystem II subunit S; RedCAP, red lineage chlorophyll a/b-binding (CAB)-like protein.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Carbon-concentrating mechanisms (CCMs)</h3><p>Previous studies of picoplanktonic Mamiellophyceae suggested that these algae might possess a C<sub>4</sub>-like carbon fixation pathway to alleviate low CO<sub>2</sub> affinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1629">49</a></sup>. A C<sub>4</sub>-like CCM has also been reported in photosynthetic stramenopiles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cock, J. M. et al. The Ectocarpus genome and the independent evolution of multicellularity in brown algae. Nature 465, 617–621 (2010)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1635">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roberts, K., Granum, E., Leegood, R. C. &amp; Raven, J. A. Carbon acquisition by diatoms. Photosynth. Res. 93, 79–88 (2007)." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1635_1">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kroth, P. G. et al. A model for carbohydrate metabolism in the diatom Phaeodactylum tricornutum deduced from comparative whole genome analysis. PLoS ONE 3, e1426 (2008)." href="#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1635_2">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Radakovits, R. et al. Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat. Commun. 3, 686 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1638">53</a></sup>. CCMs mainly rely on carbonic anhydrases (CAs) that catalyse the reversible conversion of carbon dioxide to bicarbonate. Four CA genes belonging to the delta- and gamma-type CAs were identified in the genome of <i>P. coloniale</i>, while alpha- and beta-type CAs were absent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig4">4a</a>). Among Viridiplantae, only Mamiellophyceae were found to encode delta-type CAs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">12</a>). Whereas alpha-, beta- and gamma-type CAs apparently evolved in the common ancestor of Archaeplastida (alpha- and beta-type CAs were lost in <i>P. coloniale</i>, and alpha-type CAs in the later-diverging Mamiellophyceae, perhaps related to cell miniaturization in both groups), delta-type CAs apparently evolved in the common ancestor of Viridiplantae and were independently lost in the core Chlorophyta and in Streptophyta.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="CCMs in P. coloniale."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: CCMs in <i>P. coloniale</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="332" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Maximum-likelihood phylogeny of CA proteins in <i>P. coloniale</i>. <b>b</b>, Proposed CCMs in which inorganic carbon is assimilated by <i>P. coloniale</i> based on predicted protein localizations. A brown arrow denotes that a reaction occurs only in <i>P. coloniale</i>, and a grey dotted arrow denotes a reaction that exists in Mamiellophyceae. MA, malic acid; MDH, malate dehydrogenase; ME, malic enzyme; Pyr, pyruvate; 3-PGA, 3-phosphoglyceric acid; PPDK, pyruvate, phosphate dikinase; RuBisCO, ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase oxygenase; TCA, tricarboxylic acid cycle.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Here we propose a putative model of CCMs in <i>P. coloniale</i>, based on the targets of the genes that are necessary for inorganic carbon assimilation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig4">4b</a>). As a potential C<sub>4</sub>-like CCM, malate dehydrogenase catalyses the reaction to yield malate in the cytosol, mitochondrion and chloroplast (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig4">4b</a>). Malic enzymes could be transported into the mitochondrion and chloroplast, where they release CO<sub>2</sub>. Previous studies of CCMs in <i>Micromonas</i> and <i>Ostreococcus</i> suggested that these algae might perform cytosol- and chloroplast-based C<sub>4</sub>-like CCMs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1718">49</a></sup>. <i>P. coloniale</i>, however, potentially harbours cytosol-, chloroplast- and mitochondrion-based CCMs to enhance the ability to concentrate CO<sub>2</sub> in a low-CO<sub>2</sub> environment. Interestingly, the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome encoded phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (PEPCK) but not pyruvate carboxylase (PC), opposite to the situation in the genomes of Mamiellophyceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Worden, A. Z. et al. Green evolution and dynamic adaptations revealed by genomes of the marine picoeukaryotes Micromonas. Science 324, 268–272 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1733">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Palenik, B. et al. The tiny eukaryote Ostreococcus provides genomic insights into the paradox of plankton speciation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 7705–7710 (2007)." href="#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1736">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Derelle, E. et al. Genome analysis of the smallest free-living eukaryote Ostreococcus tauri unveils many unique features. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 11647–11652 (2006)." href="#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1736_1">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Moreau, H. et al. Gene functionalities and genome structure in Bathycoccus prasinos reflect cellular specializations at the base of the green lineage. Genome Biol. 13, R74 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1739">56</a></sup>. This result suggests that a distinct CCM might exist in <i>P. coloniale</i> that uses phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP) as a substrate from the glycolytic pathway to produce oxaloacetate (OAA) by PEPCK, instead of PC as in the Mamiellophyceae (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">13</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Analysis of carbohydrate-active enzymes (CAZymes) and peptidoglycan biosynthesis</h3><p>The <i>P. coloniale</i> genome encoded 34 glycoside hydrolases (GHs) and 83 glycosyltransferases (GTs) belonging to 16 GH and 33 GT families (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">14</a>). The total number of CAZymes was lower than in the early-diverging Chlorophyta and Streptophyta, and even lower than in <i>Ostreococcus</i> spp., the smallest eukaryotes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">15</a>), which probably reflects the simple chemical structure of the <i>P. coloniale</i> cell wall (cells are enclosed within thick cell walls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Jouenne, F. et al. Prasinoderma singularis sp. nov. (Prasinophyceae, Chlorophyta), a solitary coccoid prasinophyte from the South-East Pacific Ocean. Protist 162, 70–84 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1776">57</a></sup>). <i>P. coloniale</i>, however, harbours all genes involved in the biosynthesis and metabolism of starch (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">16</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM1">11</a>). Surprisingly, we could not find any enzymes involved in the synthesis or remodelling of the major components of the primary cell wall in embryophytes, such as enzymes of cellulose, mannan, xyloglucan and xylan biosynthesis and degradation. <i>Chlorella</i> spp. have been reported to contain a cell wall with components of glucosamine polymers such as chitin and chitosan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Blanc, G. et al. The Chlorella variabilis NC64A genome reveals adaptation to photosymbiosis, coevolution with viruses, and cryptic sex. Plant Cell 22, 2943–2955 (2010)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1792">58</a></sup>. However, very few chitosan-related genes were identified in the genome of <i>P. coloniale</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">17</a>). Interestingly, some but not many bacteria/archaea-specific protein glycosylation genes could be detected in the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome, such as low-salt glycan biosynthesis protein Agl12, low-salt glycan biosynthesis reductase Agl14 and the GT AglE, which are involved in S-layer and cell surface structure biogenesis in bacteria and archaea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Fagan, R. P. &amp; Fairweather, N. F. Biogenesis and functions of bacterial S-layers. Nat. Rev. Microbiol. 12, 211–222 (2014)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1806">59</a></sup>. Furthermore, seven copies of regulatory response/sensor proteins, homologous to bacteria, could be identified in <i>P. coloniale</i>, which might respond to environmental signals. Further studies are needed to biochemically explore the main components of the cell wall of <i>P. coloniale</i>.</p><p>Peptidoglycan is the main component of cell walls in bacteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Seltmann, G. &amp; Holst, O. The Bacterial Cell Wall (Springer Science &amp; Business Media, 2013)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1819">60</a></sup>. Peptidoglycan biosynthesis requires several enzymes to participate in the conversion of UDP-<i>N</i>-acetyl-<span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-glucosamine (GlcNAc) to GlcNac-<i>N</i>-acetylmuramyl-pentapeptide-pyrophosphoryl-undecaprenol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Lovering, A. L., Safadi, S. S. &amp; Strynadka, N. C. J. Structural perspective of peptidoglycan biosynthesis and assembly. Annu. Rev. Biochem. 81, 451–478 (2012)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1832">61</a></sup>. All ten core enzymes involved in peptidoglycan biosynthesis were identified in the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig5">5a</a>). Consistent with previous results, Glaucoplantae (<i>C. paradoxa</i>) and <i>Micromonas pusilla</i> (Mamiellophyceae), as well as all streptophyte algae, bryophytes and ferns, encoded all the core enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="van Baren, M. J. et al. Evidence-based green algal genomics reveals marine diversity and ancestral characteristics of land plants. BMC Genomics 17, 267 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1849">62</a></sup>. We conclude that peptidoglycan was present in the ancestor of Archaeplastida, completely lost in Rhodoplantae but retained in the common ancestor of Viridiplantae and Glaucoplantae, and then independently lost (to different degrees) in the later-diverging Mamiellophyceae, the core Chlorophyta and the vast majority of vascular seed plants.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Analysis of peptidoglycan biosynthesis and flagellar proteins derived from the P. coloniale genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Analysis of peptidoglycan biosynthesis and flagellar proteins derived from the <i>P. coloniale</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41559-020-1221-7/MediaObjects/41559_2020_1221_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="608" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of proteins involved in the peptidoglycan biosynthetic pathway across Archaeplastida. <b>b</b>, Distribution of key flagellar proteins across Viridiplantae and Glaucoplantae.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Evolutionary analysis of flagella and sexual reproduction in <i>P. coloniale</i>
</h3><p><i>Prasinoderma coloniale</i> and other members of the recently described class Palmophyllophyceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Leliaert, F. et al. Chloroplast phylogenomic analyses reveal the deepest-branching lineage of the Chlorophyta, Palmophyllophyceae class. nov. Sci. Rep. 6, 25367 (2016)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1896">27</a></sup> have been reported to lack flagella<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Jouenne, F. et al. Prasinoderma singularis sp. nov. (Prasinophyceae, Chlorophyta), a solitary coccoid prasinophyte from the South-East Pacific Ocean. Protist 162, 70–84 (2011)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1900">57</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Miyashita, H., Ikemoto, H., Kurano, N., Miyachi, S. &amp; Chihara, M. Prasinococcus capsulatus gen. et sp. nov., a new marine coccoid prasinophyte. J. Gen. Appl. Microbiol. 39, 571–582 (1993)." href="#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1903">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hasegawa, T. et al. Prasinoderma coloniale gen. et sp. nov., a new pelagic coccoid prasinophyte from the Western Pacific Ocean. Phycologia 35, 170–176 (1996)." href="#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1903_1">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Sieburth, J. M., Keller, M. D., Johnson, P. W. &amp; Myklestad, S. M. Widespread occurrence of the oceanic ultraplankter, Prasinococcus capsulatus (Prasinophyceae), the diagnostic “Golgi‐decapore complex” and the newly described polysaccharide “capsulan”. J. Phycol. 35, 1032–1043 (1999)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1906">65</a></sup>. We performed a comparative analysis of flagellar proteins, and found that non-flagellate species (three species of Trebouxiophyceae and four of <i>Ostreococcus</i> and <i>Bathycoccus</i>) have ≤26 core flagellar proteins and a total number of flagellar proteins of ≤140 (average 117, <i>n</i> = 7), whereas flagellate species display ≥40 core flagellar proteins and a total of ≥192 flagellar proteins (average 272, <i>n</i> = 13) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#Fig5">5b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#MOESM3">18</a>). This is corroborated by recent analyses among non-flagellate organisms (angiosperms, Rhodoplantae and pennate diatoms) that yielded on average 77 flagellar proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rodríguez-Ezpeleta, N., Philippe, H., Brinkmann, H., Becker, B. &amp; Melkonian, M. Phylogenetic analyses of nuclear, mitochondrial, and plastid multigene data sets support the placement of Mesostigma in the Streptophyta. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 723–731 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1929">21</a></sup> (<i>n</i> = 10). Furthermore, non-flagellate species completely lack central pair proteins, dynein heavy chains (DHC1–7), and most of the intraflagellar transport (IFT) and radial spoke proteins (RSP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rodríguez-Ezpeleta, N., Philippe, H., Brinkmann, H., Becker, B. &amp; Melkonian, M. Phylogenetic analyses of nuclear, mitochondrial, and plastid multigene data sets support the placement of Mesostigma in the Streptophyta. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 723–731 (2007)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1936">21</a></sup>. RSP3, which binds to the inner dynein arm of the axonemal doublet microtubule and is required for axonemal sliding and flagellar motility, is also absent in non-flagellate organisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Yang, P. &amp; Smith, E. F. in The Chlamydomonas Sourcebook (ed. Witman, G. B.) 209–234 (Elsevier, 2009)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1941">66</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Jivan, A., Earnest, S., Juang, Y. C. &amp; Cobb, M. H. Radial spoke protein 3 is a mammalian protein kinase A-anchoring protein that binds ERK1/2. J. Biol. Chem. 284, 29437–29445 (2009)." href="/articles/s41559-020-1221-7#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d39070e1944">67</a></sup>. The number of flagellar proteins in <i>P. coloniale</i> (50 core proteins, 217 total flagellar proteins) and the presence of IFT (12) and DHC proteins (6), as well as RSP3, strongly suggest that <i>P. coloniale</i> can produce flagellate cells.
